### Question 1: I am writing to request your review of my course tracking in preparation for graduation. I am pursuing a double major in Computer Science and Applied Mathematics. I have attached the spreadsheet tracking my courses for your review. I would appreciate it if you could take a look and provide any necessary feedback: Humility, Global Humanities and Social Change, Linear Algebra, Modern Vietnamese Culture and Society, Quantitative Reasoning for a Digital Age, Discrete Math, Computer Science II: Data Structures, Programming for Data Science and Visualization, Design and Systems Thinking, Scientific Inquiry, Computer Organization, Calculus, Intelligent Robot Studio: From Theory to Practice, How to become a writer: lessons from Vietnamese and non-Vietnamese writers, Introduction to Political Science, The Global Vietnam War II, Deep Learning for Artificial Intelligence, Advanced Deep Learning, Probability, Computer Science I: Introduction to Programming, Algorithms and Theory of Computing, Database System, Advanced Programming Techniques, Introduction to Artificial Intelligence, Machine Learning for Data Science, Multivariable Calculus, Mathematical Statistics, General Work Program (GWP) 1, [Independent Research Project] Labeled FreSCo- Mining Frequent Patterns in labeled Simplicial Complexes, Object-Oriented Analysis and Design, Statistical Learning, Operating Systems, Abstract Algebra, Differential Equations, Stochastic Calculus, Natural Language Processing, Advanced Linear Algebra.  
### Answer: 

Based on the provided information, here's a breakdown of your courses and how they align with the Computer Science major requirements:

1. **Core Curriculum**:
   - Global Humanities (Humility, Global Humanities and Social Change): Counts as 4 credits.
   - Modern Vietnamese Culture and Society: Counts as 4 credits.
   - Design and Systems Thinking: Counts as 4 credits.
   - Quantitative Reasoning for a Digital Age: Counts as 4 credits.

2. **Exploratory Requirements**:
   - You have taken several courses that can potentially fulfill this requirement, but you should ensure that you meet the total credit hours needed for this category.

3. **Major Requirements**:
   - CS101: Introduction to Programming: Counts as 4 credits.
   - CS103: Programming for Data Science and Visualization: Counts as 4 credits.
   - CS203: Computer Organization: Counts as 4 credits.
   - CS207: Software Construction: Counts as 4 credits.
   - CS208: Machine Learning for Data Science: Counts as 4 credits.
   - CS211: Operating Systems: Counts as 4 credits.
   - CS212: Programming Language Paradigms: Counts as 4 credits.
   - CS301: Foundations of Software Engineering: Counts as 4 credits.
   - CS302: Algorithms & Theory of Computing: Counts as 4 credits.
   - CS303: Human-Computer Interaction: Counts as 4 credits.
   - CS304: Database Systems: Counts as 4 credits.
   - CS306: Computer Security: Counts as 4 credits.
   - CS307: Machine Learning: Counts as 4 credits.
   - CS310: Web Applications & Human-Computer Interaction: Counts as 4 credits.
   - CS311: Advanced Deep Learning: Counts as 4 credits.
   - CS312: Natural Language Processing: Counts as 4 credits.
   - CS313: Deep Learning for AI: Counts as 4 credits.
   - CS3xx: Bioinformatics: Counts as 4 credits.
   - MATH308: Statistical Learning: Counts as 4 credits.
   - MATH301: Optimization: Counts as 4 credits.
   - ENG301: Computer Vision: Counts as 4 credits.

4. **Double Counting**:
   - Some courses like CS208, CS307, CS311, CS312, CS313, MATH308, MATH301, ENG301 might be double-counted towards both Computer Science and Applied Mathematics. Ensure that you are not exceeding the allowed limit for double-counted credits.

5. **Capstone Project**:
   - You need to complete a capstone project which will be graded No Pass/Pass/Honors and must be evaluated by the supervising faculty at least one month before the end of your graduating term.

6. **Vietnamese Students' Extra Credits**:
   - If you are a Vietnamese student, you are required to complete military training, physical education, and political courses. These do not count toward the 128-credit requirement.

7. **English Assessment Test**:
   - If English is not your first language, you must take an English Assessment Test required by NECHE Accreditation.

Ensure that all courses are correctly categorized and that you meet all the requirements for both majors. Review the spreadsheet you've attached and verify that all courses are accounted for and properly categorized. If there are any discrepancies or missing courses, please address them accordingly. Good luck with your graduation!

### Question 2: Can you differentiate a cross-listed course and elective applied course in the case of a double major?  
### Answer: 

Yes, you can differentiate a cross-listed course and an elective applied course in the case of a double major.

Cross-listed courses are those that are shared between two majors or a major and a minor, and they can be used to satisfy both sets of requirements up to a limit of 8 credits (two courses) per pair of majors or major and minor. This means that if a student is majoring in both A and B and minoring in C, they could potentially use up to 6 double-counted courses: two in A and B, two in B and C, and two in A and C.

Elective applied courses, on the other hand, are courses from other majors that emphasize the applications of mathematics. Students must take at least 2 applied courses (at least one at the 200 level). These courses do not count towards the 8-credit limit for double-counted courses. For example, if major A requires certain courses from major B for out-of-area foundation, exploration, or applications of the major, and these courses are not cross-listed between A and B, they do not count toward the 8-credit limit.

In summary, cross-listed courses can be used to satisfy both sets of requirements for a double major up to a limit of 8 credits, while elective applied courses cannot be used to satisfy the double major requirements and must meet specific criteria and prerequisites. It's important to consult with an academic advisor for course selection and approval.

### Question 3: I would like to ask for the capstone withdrawal policy. What will be the impact when I choose to drop the capstone before the Fall 2025 term starts? Will there be any penalties associated with withdrawing, such as a 'W' notation on my transcript?  
### Answer: 

According to the provided context, the capstone withdrawal policy follows the general course withdrawal rules. For the Fall 2025 term, the first withdrawal period is from after 4:00 PM Friday of Week 2 to 4:00 PM Friday of Week 4. During this period, you can withdraw from the course without any consequences, and no "W" grade will be recorded on your transcript.

If you choose to withdraw from the capstone after the halfway point of the course (end of Week 4 for an 8-week course or end of Week 8 for a full-term course), you will receive a grade of 'F', which will affect your GPA. However, exceptions can be made in exceptional circumstances, requiring supporting documentation and authorization from your course instructor, academic/major advisor, and the Registrar's Office.

Since the capstone is being withdrawn before the Fall 2025 term starts, it falls within the first withdrawal period, meaning no "W" grade will be recorded on your transcript. There will be no penalties associated with withdrawing during this period. However, ensure you follow the proper procedure by submitting the Course Withdrawal form to the Registrar's Office within the specified timeframe. 

Please note that this policy applies to all courses, including the capstone, unless otherwise specified. If you have any specific questions or concerns regarding the capstone withdrawal process, it is advisable to consult with your Academic/Major Advisor.

### Question 4: Questions related to course history, in which one course can have multiple IDs? Like Advanced Deep Learning course. How would it be handled among different cohorts?  
### Answer: 

When a course like Advanced Deep Learning has multiple IDs and is offered to different cohorts, it typically means that the course content may evolve over time due to updates, improvements, or changes in the curriculum. Here's how it could be handled:

1. **Course Versioning**: Each version of the course could be given a unique ID, indicating the specific iteration of the course content. For example, Advanced Deep Learning v1.0, Advanced Deep Learning v2.0, etc.

2. **Cohort-Specific Content**: Different cohorts might receive slightly different versions of the course material, especially if there are significant updates between offerings. This ensures that each cohort gets the most up-to-date information relevant to their learning experience.

3. **Consistent Core Material**: While the course may change, there will usually be core material that remains consistent across all versions. This ensures that students from different cohorts can still compare notes and share knowledge effectively.

4. **Documentation and Resources**: The institution might maintain detailed documentation about each version of the course, including what was added, removed, or modified. This helps students understand the differences between versions and plan accordingly.

5. **Feedback Loop**: Students' feedback on previous versions of the course can influence future iterations, ensuring continuous improvement. This iterative process helps in refining the course content for all future cohorts.

In summary, handling multiple IDs for the same course among different cohorts involves maintaining clear distinctions between versions, ensuring consistent core material, and leveraging feedback to improve the course over time. This approach allows each cohort to benefit from the latest developments while still having access to reliable and comparable educational resources.

### Answer: 

Yes, if you want to declare a Computer Science major, you typically need to complete the required courses, including Computer Science I, unless you have waived it according to specific policies. The context provided does not mention waiving Computer Science I, so generally, you would need to take it to meet the major requirements. However, policies regarding waivers can vary, so it's best to check with your academic advisor for confirmation.

The Computer Science

[Source: CS_major_handbook.pdf, Page: 12]
course (4 credits) Major Outline: Curricular Structure and Progression All students are encouraged to take the course Introduction to Computer Science ﬁrst to explore all diﬀerent areas of computer science. Then the students are required to take ﬁve courses that will lay out the foundation of programming, mathematics, and hardware. After having the knowledge from these foundation courses, the students will continue their journey with three intermediate courses, which are designed to cover the important and basic knowledge in the major aspects in computer science. Following the completion of their foundation and intermediate courses, students will have ﬂexibility in their choice of four elective advanced courses to explore their areas of interest deeper. Finally, in the fourth year, students can choose to do the capstone to graduate with honors or to take two more intermediate or advanced courses to graduate without honors. ### Question 7: If I have taken the following courses: Mathematical Statistics, Abstract Algebra, Linear Algebra, Multivariable Calculus, Calculus, Differential Equation, Stochastic Calculus, Statistical Learning, Discrete Mathematics, Probability, am I eligible to declare a minor in Applied Mathematics? What about majoring in Applied Mathematics?  
### Answer: 

Based on the information provided, you have taken several courses that align with the requirements for both a minor and a major in Applied Mathematics.

For a minor in Applied Mathematics:
- You have taken Linear Algebra, Calculus, and Statistics (Mathematical Statistics and Statistical Learning).
- You have taken Intermediate courses like Differential Equations, Discrete Mathematics, Probability, and Stochastic Calculus.
- You have taken Advanced courses like Stochastic Calculus and Statistical Learning.

Since you have met the minimum requirement of taking at least 2 advanced courses, you are eligible to declare a minor in Applied Mathematics.

For a major in Applied Mathematics:
- You have taken all the required foundation courses (Linear Algebra, Calculus, Introduction to Statistics, Multivariable Calculus).
- You have taken 2 intermediate courses (Differential Equations and Discrete Mathematics).
- You have taken 2 advanced courses (Stochastic Calculus and Statistical Learning).

However, to major in Applied Mathematics, you need to complete additional courses, including a Pure Mathematics course and a Research Seminar. Additionally, you need to consult with an academic advisor for course selection and approval.

In summary, you are eligible to declare a minor in Applied Mathematics, but you still need to complete additional courses and consult with an academic advisor to major in Applied Mathematics.

Since you have only completed two foundational courses, you have not yet met the requirement of completing all 100-level major requirements. Therefore, you are not currently eligible to start a Capstone in your major Arts and Media Studies. You need to ensure that all 100-level major requirements are complete before applying for the capstone. Additionally, you should aim for a major GPA of at least 3.6 to meet the eligibility criteria. Once these conditions are met, you can apply for the capstone after consulting with your prospective advisor.

### Question 11: I have a question if I take a gap year, will my financial aid be canceled? If yes, can I still re-apply for the financial aid program when I get back to school?  
### Answer: 

Taking a gap year itself does not directly result in the cancellation of your financial aid. However, your financial aid eligibility could be affected depending on the circumstances of your absence and whether you maintain your enrollment status as a full-time student upon returning.

If you are approved for a leave of absence, including a gap year, and you return to Fulbright within the allowed timeframe, your financial aid should generally be reinstated. You may need to reapply for financial aid, but it is likely that you will be eligible for continued support based on your previous award.

It's important to communicate with the financial aid office at Fulbright regarding your plans and any changes in your circumstances. They can provide guidance specific to your situation and ensure that you understand the requirements for maintaining your financial aid eligibility. Always check the "Fees & Charges Guide" for detailed information on tuition credits and reductions due to leaves of absence.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 11]
student must meet with the Dean’s Office, who establishes the conditions that the student must meet prior to returning to Fulbright. While on suspension, a student may not visit the Fulbright campus unless permission is given from the Dean’s Office or the Vice President of Student Affairs for the purpose of attending relevant meetings. Suspensions typically are for one term or one academic year. A student may be suspended a maximum of two times. A student will not be suspended a third time but will instead have their enrolment in the program canceled. A student in this situation must apply for re-admission. The student must include with his or her application for re-admission a letter that addresses the reasons for their suspensions and cancellation, and the measures they have taken to address those reasons. The re-admission decision is made by the Dean’s Office in consultation with the appropriate members of faculty and staff. Students who are re-admitted must also re- apply for financial aid.

If a student determines that medical leave would be beneficial, the student must submit the Undergraduate Leave of Absence form and the Fulbright Impact Assessment form, along with all medical supporting documents, before the last day of class. After the approved period of absence, a student is put on Administrative Leave of Absence (Admin LOA) for a maximum of one term if the Registrar’s Office receives no response of return confirmation or no registration in the returning term. After one term of Admin LOA, a student is officially withdrawn from Fulbright unless they have contacted the Registrar’s Office and confirmed the plan to return by registering for course(s) or have been granted a leave extension, a maximum of another term. The extension must be endorsed by the Registrar’s Office and approved by the Dean’s Office.

### Question 13: If I major in Economics, is it sufficient that I take Scholar Development to fulfill Experiential Learning requirements?  
### Answer: 

No, taking Scholar Development alone is not sufficient to fulfill the Experiential Learning requirements for an Economics major at Fulbright. According to the provided information, students must complete at least 04 credits and a maximum of 12 credits under the Experiential Learning Program (ELP) as a graduation requirement. The ELP offers streams within Academic Affairs (AA), Career Services (CS), and Center for Entrepreneurship and Innovation (CEI), but Scholar Development does not specifically mention being part of these streams. Therefore, it would be necessary to consult with an advisor to determine which courses count towards the ELP requirements for the Economics major. Scholar Development may or may not count towards these requirements depending on its content and structure. It's important to ensure that any courses taken meet the criteria set by the ELP for fulfilling the experiential learning component of the degree.

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 20]
count toward elective or major requirements (upon advisor approval). Fulbright’s Experiential Learning Program (ELP) exists to create, standardize, and facilitate academic credit-bearing experiential learning experiences. All full-time, undergraduate, degree- seeking students must complete at least 04 credits and a maximum of 12 credits under the ELP as a graduation requirement. Current ELP streams are housed within Academic Affairs (AA), Career Services (CS), and Center for Entrepreneurship and Innovation (CEI), where each team is responsible for administrative and academic duties of their respective stream(s). The ELP operates within Fulbright’s undergraduate program’s standard operating procedures and is developed in consultation with undergraduate program leadership and faculty. ### Question 15: Can I take all four engineering foundation courses and only two intermediate engineering courses?  
### Answer: 

Yes, you can take all four engineering foundation courses and only two intermediate engineering courses. The context states that students will take at least 2 intermediate courses, but it does not specify a maximum limit. Therefore, it is possible to take more than the minimum required number of intermediate courses. However, it is recommended to complete the foundation courses as early as possible.

been exposed to understanding the users' needs, defining engineering problems, and ideating potential solutions before gaining some hands-on experience creating, making, and testing a physical product. Students interested in exploring further can take several Foundation Engineering courses, which are designed specifically to bridge engineering practice, mathematics, and basic sciences. In general, these courses only require the core engineering course as the prerequisite, if any, making them accessible to undetermined-major students to pursue courses of interest. Once the students declare their major or minor in Engineering, they will gain more specialized knowledge and skills by taking more Engineering Foundation courses and Engineering Advanced courses. As part of a human-centered approach that aims to determine human-centric problems and then apply innovative engineering solutions, the students will be encouraged to take Elective courses in Arts, Humanities, Social Sciences, and Entrepreneurship. The Advanced-level courses and two-semester Capstone projects provide students with deep knowledge and a chance to apply what they have learned into real-world problems. In addition, students must complete a well-balanced set of courses in mathematics, natural sciences, arts, humanities, and social sciences to be awarded a bachelor's degree in engineering. Although students will be required to take half of the minimum number of credits for graduation in engineering, they will have a certain degree of freedom of choosing   elective courses to pursue their interest of study with engineering. CS204: Introduction to Artiﬁcial Intelligence Prerequisites: Take at least one 100-level CS course This course introduces students to the basic knowledge on Artiﬁcial intelligence.Artiﬁcial intelligence (AI) is a research ﬁeld that studies how to realize the intelligent human behaviors on a computer. The ultimate goal of AI is to make a computer that can learn, plan, and solve problems autonomously. In this course, student will learn the foundational principles and practice implementing some of these applications including representation, problem solving, and learning methods of artiﬁcial intelligence. Accordingly, students should be able to develop intelligent systems by assembling solutions to concrete

[Source: CS_major_handbook.pdf, Page: 19]
(AI) is a research ﬁeld that studies how to realize the intelligent human behaviors on a computer. The ultimate goal of AI is to make a computer that can learn, plan, and solve problems autonomously. In this course, student will learn the foundational principles and practice implementing some of these applications including representation, problem solving, and learning methods of artiﬁcial intelligence. Accordingly, students should be able to develop intelligent systems by assembling solutions to concrete computational problems; understand the role of knowledge representation, problem solving, and learning in intelligent-system engineering; and appreciate the role of problem solving, vision, and language in understanding human intelligence from a computational perspective. ### Question 17: Does completing both Microeconomics and Macroeconomics satisfy the core theory requirement, or is an additional course needed?  
### Answer: 

Completing both Microeconomics and Macroeconomics satisfies the core theory requirement for the Economics Major. According to the provided information, students should take Economic Methods and Principles of Economics 1 and 2 before taking either Intermediate level courses, Econometrics, Microeconomic Analysis, or Macroeconomics Analysis. This implies that Principles of Economics 1 and 2 cover the core theory aspects of microeconomics and macroeconomics. Therefore, no additional course is needed beyond these two introductory courses to meet the core theory requirement.

### Question 18: If I want to major in Engineering, can I choose other programming courses besides Computer Science I and Computer Science II, for instance Introduction to data visualization?  
### Answer: 

Based on the provided information, it seems that the Engineering major does not explicitly restrict the choice of programming courses to only Computer Science I and Computer Science II. However, the context suggests that these two courses are part of the Computer Science curriculum. 

If you're looking to major in Engineering and considering taking a course like Introduction to Data Visualization, you should check the specific requirements and electives for your Engineering major program. It's possible that such a course could count towards your electives or fulfill a specific requirement within your major, but this would depend on the particular Engineering program you are enrolled in.

In summary, while the provided information does not explicitly state that you cannot take other programming courses, you should verify the specific requirements of your Engineering major program to ensure that a course like Introduction to Data Visualization aligns with those requirements. If it's an elective, then it might be a viable option. Otherwise, you may need to consult with an academic advisor to confirm.

Prerequisites: None The Programming for Data Science and Visualization course aims at providing students with a set of skills to analyze the nature of data across diﬀerent domains. The course demonstrates data visualization by understanding, questioning, and problematizing how data are generated, analyzed, and used. The students will be able to apply concepts and skills to visualize data, interpret the ﬁndings, and examine the impacts of data-driven decision. The course will also get the students familiar with Python programming language and its libraries to analyze, visualize and discover data insight leading to a proper data-driven decision-making process. CS104: Introduction to Computer Science Prerequisites: None The course Introduction to Computer Science aims at teaching students the basics of computer science, programming, and data science, along with potential career paths in the ﬁelds. Computer science is the study of how computers work and how to use them for diﬀerent purposes. Programming is the

[Source: CS_major_handbook.pdf, Page: 5]
of the most quickly emerging and innovative technology economies in the world – and beyond. As part of the major, students will be equipped with the foundational knowledge in Computer Science and relevant disciplines. They will be exposed to essential areas of the CS discipline including theory, systems, and applications. They will learn about the underlying mathematical ideas that are critical for computation, establish proficiency in the process of designing systems and applications, gain experience in collecting and analyzing data using modern technologies, and begin to develop an understanding for the role of users in the design of systems and applications. Courses in Computer Science go beyond content to help students learn through direct experiences in projects and problems. Students will also have the opportunity to further focus their studies by selecting a concentration, such as software engineering, artificial intelligence and machine learning, data science, business analytics. The Computer Science

[Source: CS_major_handbook.pdf, Page: 19]
Prerequisites: None (CS104 is recommended) This course aims to equip students with the skills necessary to solve computational problems using a high-level programming language. Students will develop their abilities to use (i) fundamental programming constructs such as branching statements, loops, functions, and recursion and (ii) abstract data types such as lists and maps. Students will also be exposed to basic concepts of object-oriented programming. The course demonstrates data visualization by understanding, questioning, and problematizing how data are generated, analyzed, and used. The students will be able to apply concepts and skills to visualize data, interpret the ﬁndings, and examine the impacts of data-driven decision. The course will also get the students familiar

### Question 19: Given I took Discrete Mathematics in Spring 2022 and earned B, then the same course in Spring 2024 and earned C-, which grades will be kept?  
### Answer: 

The higher grade, which is the B from Spring 2022, will be kept and used in the GPA calculation. The lower grade, which is the C- from Spring 2024, will be overwritten by the notation "RP", meaning "repeated", and remain on the transcript. The credits of the repeated course are counted toward the total attempted credits. However, the student should consult with their Academic/Major Advisor about their program plan if they are considering taking the class for a third time.

### Question 21: Can I register for more than 20 credits in a semester?  
### Answer: 

No, you cannot register for more than 20 credits in a semester. The guidelines state that students are required to register for no fewer than 12 credit hours and no more than 20 credit hours per term. Therefore, registering for more than 20 credits would exceed the allowed maximum. If you wish to take more than 20 credits, you would need to submit a specific form requesting permission, but such requests must be approved by the Academic/Major Advisor before submission.

In order to make standard progress toward completing the degree, students are required to register for no fewer than 12 credit hours and no more than 20 credit hours per term. and a minor) share cross- listed or tagged1 courses, up to 8 credits (two courses) can be used to satisfy both sets of requirements. This limit is per each pair of majors (or major and minor) and does not restrict the total number of double-counted credits or courses a student can claim. For example, a student can major in both A and B and minor in C with a total of 6 double-counted courses: two in A and B, two in B and C, and two in A and C. This rule does not apply to courses that are not cross-listed or tagged. For instance, if major A requires certain courses from major B for out-of-area foundation, exploration, or applications of the major, and these courses are not cross-listed between A and B, they do not count toward the 8-credit limit. 1 In interdisciplinary minors, the requirements mainly consist of courses from other majors. ### Question 22: What is the maximum number of times I can retake a course to improve my grade, and how does it affect my transcript?  
### Answer: 

According to the provided information, a student may retake the same course up to three times. The first two retakes are generally allowed under certain conditions, such as receiving grades of 'D+', 'D', 'F', or 'NP' for the course, or withdrawing and receiving a grade of 'W'. In special cases, a student may also request to repeat a course after receiving a grade higher than a 'D+' following consultation with the student's academic/major advisor, the course instructor, and the Registrar's Office.

If a student repeats a course, the higher grade will be kept and used in the GPA calculation, while the lower grade will be overwritten by the notation "RP" (meaning "repeated") and remain on the transcript. Credits of the repeated course are counted toward the total attempted credits. However, a student may not retake the same course for the third time unless they failed the second time or if a specific grade is needed to meet the major requirements. 

In summary, the maximum number of times a student can retake a course to improve their grade is three, but the third retake is only allowed under specific circumstances. The repeated grades and their notations will appear on the transcript, but the higher grade will be used in the GPA calculation.

### Question 23: For a student doing the literature capstone, if a student includes charts or tables, do they count toward the word limit?

Charts or tables do not count toward the word limit for a student doing the literature capstone. The guidelines specify that images with captions are not included in the word count.

### Question 24: If I failed Capstone I in the Fall 2024, can I redo it in Spring 2025?  
### Answer: 

Based on the provided context, there is no explicit mention of retaking Capstone I if you fail it. Typically, academic programs have specific policies regarding retakes, which are usually outlined in the course syllabus or university handbook. Since the context does not provide such information, it is advisable to consult the relevant academic advisor or the Capstone Handbook for Spring 2025 to understand the specific procedures and policies related to retaking Capstone I. Therefore, the answer is that you cannot definitively conclude whether you can redo Capstone I in Spring 2025 without checking the official policies.

### Question 25: If I do Literature capstone in Decolonial studies, can it be approved?  
### Answer: 

If you do a Literature capstone in Decolonial studies, it may not be approved because there is no current faculty expertise listed under decolonial studies in the provided information. The approved fields include modern Vietnamese literature and history, literature of the environment, early modern and contemporary poetry, Asian diasporic literature, creative writing, translation, gender and sexuality studies, postcolonial studies, and medieval, Renaissance, and classical studies. Since decolonial studies is not explicitly mentioned among these fields, it could potentially lead to the application not being approved. However, it's important to consult early on with a prospective advisor to determine the suitability and feasibility of the proposed project.

1. General Information: The Literature Capstone is a culmination of a student’s curriculum through an individual project involving independent research. Through support from existing faculty, students propose, undertake, and write up a research or creative writing project over the course of two consecutive semesters, labelled as Capstone I and Capstone II. Students are encouraged to conduct primary and secondary research, even for creative projects. Fieldwork, however, is not expected at this level. Students are also expected to think very carefully about an appropriate scope of research and production plan for the proposed capstone. ### Question 27: Can Discrete Mathematics be counted for both Computer Science and Applied Mathematics majors?  
### Answer: 

Yes, Discrete Mathematics can be counted for both Computer Science and Applied Mathematics majors. The context provided states that MATH 202: Discrete Math (4 credits) is also counted towards the Computer Science major requirements. Since no restrictions are mentioned regarding double-counting this course for the Applied Mathematics major, it can be assumed that it can also be counted towards the Applied Mathematics major. Therefore, Discrete Mathematics can be used to fulfill requirements for both majors.

APPLIED MATHEMATICS: Description Applied Mathematics is the study and application of mathematical concepts and methods to solve complex problems in a wide range of areas such as natural science, engineering, architecture, business, industry and even psychology and social science. Its omnipresent power to deliver optimum answers is felt and enjoyed in many aspects of life from apparently simple activities such as booking a Grab trip and using the suggestion feature of Netflix to grander-scale projects such as bridge building and sending satellites into space. A close- knit family with data science and artificial intelligence, Applied Mathematics unleashes the enormous computing power that could solve unlimited conventional and unconventional problems that arise from natural phenomena and social needs. At Fulbright University Vietnam, the Applied Mathematics major offers a unique approach that is both theoretically rigorous and highly interactive in a truly interdisciplinary environment. The major will provide

### Question 28: What steps should I take to ensure a smooth transfer of my exchange semester credits from Bard College to Fulbright?  
### Answer: 

To ensure a smooth transfer of your exchange semester credits from Bard College to Fulbright, you should follow these steps:

1. Review the Transfer Credit Policy V1.0 published in the One-Stop portal to understand the process and requirements.

2. Gather all necessary information about the courses you want to transfer, including course curriculum, learning outcomes, course content, and assessments.

3. Submit an Application for Credit Transfer to the Office of the Registrar at Fulbright University Vietnam, either by sending an official transcript with seal from Bard College directly to the Office of the Registrar at the email registrar@fulbright.edu.vn or the mailing address provided in the policy.

4. Consult with your Academic/Major Advisor about your program plan if you are considering taking the class for a third time.

5. Ensure that the information provided in the Application for Credit Transfer is complete and correct, and sign the Declaration section of the form.

6. Await coordination between the Registrar and the Office of the Dean to determine whether credit or an exemption is justified.

7. Keep track of final grades and ensure they meet any specific grade requirements for meeting major requirements. If you need further assistance, you can always reach out to your Academic/Major Advisor.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 10]
courses delivered by Fulbright. When applying for Credit Transfer, a student is responsible for providing all the information that is requested about the course(s) for which they are applying to transfer. Information may include but not limited to course curriculum, learning outcomes, course content, and assessments. To apply for Credit Transfer or an Exemption, students should submit an Application for Credit Transfer, along with the required information on the course(s), to the Office of the Registrar. The Registrar will coordinate with the Office of the Dean to determine whether credit or an exemption is justified. Credit Transfer from A-level, Advanced Placement (AP), International Baccalaureate (IB), and Cambridge International Exam (CIE) courses will be determined according to the AP/IB/CIE Credit Policy (in development). For more information, please refer to the Transfer Credit policy_Final_V3.0 published in the One Stop portal.

A student who has earned credit at another school or university may apply to have that credit counted towards their Fulbright undergraduate degree. This is referred to as Credit Transfer. In addition, a student who has credit for a course passed at another university that is evaluated to be equivalent to a course at Fulbright may be given an Exemption from being required to do that Fulbright course. ### Question 29: Are 100-level and 200-level courses that are not explicitly categorized as E1, E2, E3, or E4 also excluded from fulfilling the Exploratory requirement?  
### Answer: 

Yes, 100-level and 200-level courses that are not explicitly categorized as E1, E2, E3, or E4 are also excluded from fulfilling the Exploratory requirement. According to the context provided, students need to complete 8 credits (2 courses at the 100-level or 200-level) chosen from each of the three categories: E1- Arts and Humanities, E2-Social Sciences, and STEM. This implies that only courses explicitly categorized under these three categories can be used to fulfill the Exploratory requirement. Courses not categorized as such do not count towards this requirement.

### Question 30: I am a Co25 student pursuing a Computer Science major, can I choose to follow either the old flowchart or the new one?  
### Answer: 

Based on the information provided, it appears that there are two different flowcharts mentioned - one for "Core Curriculum" and another for "Sample Minor Plan". However, since you're pursuing a Computer Science major, the relevant flowchart would be the "Core Curriculum" one.

The flowchart details the courses required for the Computer Science major, including both the old and new plans. Therefore, as a Co25 student pursuing a Computer Science major, you can indeed choose to follow either the old flowchart or the new one, depending on your preference and the specific requirements of your program.

Please note that the exact choice might depend on factors like the academic year you started your major, any changes in curriculum, and the specific policies of your institution. It's always best to consult with your academic advisor for the most accurate and up-to-date information.

Prerequisites: None (CS104 is recommended) This course aims to equip students with the skills necessary to solve computational problems using a high-level programming language. Students will develop their abilities to use (i) fundamental programming constructs such as branching statements, loops, functions, and recursion and (ii) abstract data types such as lists and maps. Students will also be exposed to basic concepts of object-oriented programming. CS103: Programming for Data Science and Visualization Prerequisites: None The Programming for Data Science and Visualization course aims at providing students with a set of skills to analyze the nature of data across diﬀerent domains. The course demonstrates data visualization by understanding, questioning, and problematizing how data are generated, analyzed, and used. The students will be able to apply concepts and skills to visualize data, interpret the ﬁndings, and examine the impacts of data-driven decision. The course will also get the students familiar

[Source: CS_major_handbook.pdf, Page: 5]
of the most quickly emerging and innovative technology economies in the world – and beyond. As part of the major, students will be equipped with the foundational knowledge in Computer Science and relevant disciplines. They will be exposed to essential areas of the CS discipline including theory, systems, and applications. They will learn about the underlying mathematical ideas that are critical for computation, establish proficiency in the process of designing systems and applications, gain experience in collecting and analyzing data using modern technologies, and begin to develop an understanding for the role of users in the design of systems and applications. Courses in Computer Science go beyond content to help students learn through direct experiences in projects and problems. Students will also have the opportunity to further focus their studies by selecting a concentration, such as software engineering, artificial intelligence and machine learning, data science, business analytics. ### Question 31: If I withdraw from a course after the 4th week of the semester, will I be deducted 4 credits of financial aid?  
### Answer: 

No, you will not be deducted 4 credits of financial aid if you withdraw from a course after the 4th week of the semester. The context states that no reduction in tuition is granted to students whose course withdrawal results in fewer than 12 academic credits. Therefore, the withdrawal will not affect your financial aid eligibility as long as you maintain at least 12 academic credits.

### Question 32: Why do I need to take 8 exploratory courses?  
### Answer: 

You need to take 8 exploratory courses to step out of your comfort zone and explore broad areas of study. This helps you discover more fully where your interests and passions lie. These courses are designed to cover three main categories: E1- Arts and Humanities, E2-Social Sciences, and STEM. Each category requires you to complete 2 courses at the 100-level or 200-level. No course can be taken to fulfill more than one category. Up to two of these 8 exploratory credits can be counted towards your major requirements. The goal is for you to complete these exploratory requirements by the end of your second year. However, it's important to note that class of 2023 students are exempted from this requirement. For classes of 2024, 2025, 2026, and 2027, there are four exploratory categories, and specific credit requirements apply.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 20]
and passions lie. Students need to complete 8 credits (2 courses at the 100-level or 200-level) chosen from each of the three categories: E1- Arts and Humanities, E2-Social Sciences, and STEM. No course can be taken to fulfill more than one category. At most, 8 exploratory credits can be taken as Pass/No Pass. Up to two 8 exploratory credits (2 courses) can be counted towards the student’s major requirements. If a student has more than one major, the 8-credit limit is independently applied to each major. Students should aim to complete the Exploratory requirements by the end of their second year. ### Question 33: Can I take 160 credits in 4 years given that I pay for the full tuition fee?  
### Answer: 

Based on the provided context, a student typically registers for no fewer than 12 credit hours and no more than 16 credit hours per term. Over four years, assuming two terms per year, this would mean a range of 96 to 128 credit hours. Taking 160 credits exceeds the maximum allowed per the institution's guidelines. Therefore, it is not possible to take 160 credits in 4 years under these conditions, even if you pay the full tuition fee. The number of credits you can take is regulated by the institution's policies. 

The context also mentions that a student must earn a minimum of 128 credit hours to graduate with an undergraduate degree from Fulbright. Since 160 credits exceed this minimum requirement, you could technically complete your degree with more credits, but you cannot exceed the maximum allowed per term without special approval. Thus, while you might be able to take more than the typical 16 credit hours per term, you cannot take 160 credits in total over four years without exceeding the institution's limits. The payment of full tuition fees does not affect the number of credits you can take per term.

<summary>References</summary>

A student who has earned credit at another school or university may apply to have that credit counted towards their Fulbright undergraduate degree. This is referred to as Credit Transfer. In addition, a student who has credit for a course passed at another university that is evaluated to be equivalent to a course at Fulbright may be given an Exemption from being required to do that Fulbright course. A maximum of 64 credit hours can be transferred and credited to a Fulbright undergraduate degree. When applying for Credit Transfer, a student is responsible for providing all the information that is requested about the course(s) for which they are applying to transfer. ### Question 34: What are the requirements for mathematics courses to pursue Human-Centered Engineering major?  
### Answer: 

To pursue the Human-Centered Engineering major, you need to complete a well-balanced set of courses in mathematics or basic sciences, totaling at least 24 credits. Specifically, out of these, at least 3 mathematics courses should be completed, amounting to 12 credits. Pass/No-pass grades are acceptable for these courses. This ensures a strong foundation in mathematics, which is crucial for the engineering curriculum.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Major Descriptions and Flowcharts_OfficialVersion.pdf, Page: 46]
been exposed to understanding the users' needs, defining engineering problems, and ideating potential solutions before gaining some hands-on experience creating, making, and testing a physical product. Students interested in exploring further can take several Foundation Engineering courses, which are designed specifically to bridge engineering practice, mathematics, and basic sciences. In general, these courses only require the core engineering course as the prerequisite, if any, making them accessible to undetermined-major students to pursue courses of interest. Once the students declare their major or minor in Engineering, they will gain more specialized knowledge and skills by taking more Engineering Foundation courses and Engineering Advanced courses. As part of a human-centered approach that aims to determine human-centric problems and then apply innovative engineering solutions, the students will be encouraged to take Elective courses in Arts, Humanities, Social Sciences, and Entrepreneurship. In some

[Source: Major Descriptions and Flowcharts_OfficialVersion.pdf, Page: 46]
universities or industry Research and Development (R&D) facilities Learning Outcomes Developed based on the whole-person educational framework of Fulbright University Vietnam and for seeking ABET accreditation, Human-Centered Engineering curriculum aims at supporting the development of graduates who will demonstrate: 1. An ability to identify, formulate, and solve complex engineering problems by applying principles of engineering, science, and mathematics (LO1), 2. An ability to apply engineering design to produce solutions that meet specified needs with consideration of public health, safety, and welfare, as well as global, cultural, social, environmental, and economic factors (LO2), 3. An ability to communicate effectively with a range of audiences (LO3), 4. An ability to recognize ethical and professional responsibilities in engineering situations and make informed judgments, which must consider the impact of engineering solutions in global, economic, environmental, and societal contexts (LO4), 5. An

[Source: Major Descriptions and Flowcharts_OfficialVersion.pdf, Page: 46]

The engineering curriculum is human-centered, multidisciplinary, project-based, future-fit approach while being compatible with the international engineering accreditation ABET (Accreditation Board for Engineering and Technology). The Engineering major courses are designed and developed to provide students with a broad knowledge base of multiple specializations, including Electrical Engineering, Computer Engineering, Mechanical Engineering, Control Theory (Robotics) and Entrepreneurship. The sequence of courses on each of these Engineering specializations range from Foundation level (i.e., 100-level) through Intermediate level (i.e., 200- level) to Advanced level (i.e., 300-level). The Advanced-level courses and two-semester Capstone projects provide students with deep knowledge and a chance to apply what they have learned into real-world problems. ### Question 35: How is the grade of a replaced course displayed on the transcript? Will it replace the old one or create a new entry in the transcript?  
### Answer: 

The grade of a replaced course is displayed on the transcript by keeping the higher grade and using it in the GPA calculation. The lower grade is overwritten by the notation "RP", meaning "repeated", and remains on the transcript. Therefore, it does not create a new entry but keeps the old one with an additional notation.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 10]
advisor, the course instructor, and the Registrar’s Office. Considerations will include course availability and capacity in the next academic term or next academic year. A higher grade will be kept and used in the GPA calculation. The lower grade will be overwritten by the notation “RP”, meaning “repeated”, and remain on the transcript. Credits of the repeated course are counted toward the total attempted credits. A student may not retake the same course for the third time unless he/she failed the second time or if a specific grade is needed to meet the major requirements. Students shall consult with their Academic/Major Advisor about their program plan if they are considering taking the class for a third time. 4.2 Final Grade Release Final grades will be posted by the Registrar’s Office and accessible via the One Stop portal. 4.3 Credit Transfer and Exemptions earned from other universities. ### Question 36: What are the courses that can replace the capstone for a given major?  
### Answer: 

For the Integrated Sciences (IS) major, students who choose not to do the capstone can replace it with 8 credits from IS courses, including at least 4 credits of 300-level course(s). This means they can substitute the capstone with advanced courses within the IS curriculum to meet their graduation requirements.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>

### Question 37: Is it possible to graduate with double majors in Human-Centered Engineering and Applied Mathematics? If yes, please provide a sample study plan.  
### Answer: 

Yes, it is possible to graduate with double majors in Human-Centered Engineering and Applied Mathematics. However, the specific study plan would depend on the university's requirements and the individual student's schedule.

Sample Study Plan:
1. First two years: Focus on foundational courses in both majors, including core engineering courses, mathematics, and statistics.
2. Third year: Take specialized courses in Human-Centered Engineering and Applied Mathematics, such as Foundation Engineering courses and electives in Arts, Humanities, Social Sciences, and Entrepreneurship.
3. Fourth year: Complete advanced courses in both majors, including Engineering Advanced courses and Capstone research in Applied Mathematics.
4. Fifth year (if required): Take additional courses or complete a thesis/project in either major to meet graduation requirements.

This is just a sample plan and should be adjusted according to the specific requirements of the university and the student's interests and goals. It's important to consult with academic advisors to create a personalized study plan.

APPLIED MATHEMATICS: Description Applied Mathematics is the study and application of mathematical concepts and methods to solve complex problems in a wide range of areas such as natural science, engineering, architecture, business, industry and even psychology and social science. Its omnipresent power to deliver optimum answers is felt and enjoyed in many aspects of life from apparently simple activities such as booking a Grab trip and using the suggestion feature of Netflix to grander-scale projects such as bridge building and sending satellites into space. A close- knit family with data science and artificial intelligence, Applied Mathematics unleashes the enormous computing power that could solve unlimited conventional and unconventional problems that arise from natural phenomena and social needs. At Fulbright University Vietnam, the Applied Mathematics major offers a unique approach that is both theoretically rigorous and highly interactive in a truly interdisciplinary environment. ### Question 39: What are the exchange policies of Fulbright?  
### Answer: 

Fulbright's exchange policies include provisions for personal and medical leaves. For personal reasons, students can apply for a leave of absence, but they must meet with their academic advisor to plan for making up missed credits. Fulbright guarantees reinstatement for students who take a leave of up to one academic year. For medical reasons, students may consider taking a voluntary medical leave if their physical and/or mental health concerns significantly interfere with their studies. Additionally, students participating in the Experiential Learning Program (ELP) are expected to follow specific guidelines, including submitting a summary of the program or activity, proof of attendance, and a reflection paper upon return. These policies aim to support students' well-being and academic progress while ensuring the continuity of their education. However, it's important to note that a leave does not clear the student's bill, and they may lose eligibility for scholarships. Students are also responsible for informing the Office of the Registrar upon their return and registering for classes using the One-Stop platform for the term they return to. The policies reflect Fulbright's commitment to supporting its students and promoting a student-centered learning environment.

### Question 40: What actions are considered plagiarism? Are AI-generated works counted?  
### Answer: 

Presenting text, images, or other content created by others or by generative AI as your own work, even at the sentence level, is considered plagiarism and grounds for failure of Capstone I and/or Capstone II. Yes, AI-generated works are counted and must be fully documented according to the guidelines provided.

### Question 41: Why is the graduation requirement 128 credits, but the major requirement is only 48 credits?  
### Answer: 

The graduation requirement is 128 credits because it includes both the major requirements (56 credits) and additional electives and general education courses such as the core curriculum, which consist of courses like Global Humanities, Modern Vietnamese Culture and Society, Design and Systems Thinking, and courses in the QUEST group. The major requirement is only 48 credits because it specifies the minimum credits needed to complete the Computer Science major, which includes foundational courses, intermediate courses, advanced courses, and capstone projects. The difference accounts for the other required courses and credits needed to fulfill the broader degree requirements beyond just the major.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 20]
major or minor requirements must be taken for a letter grade. Students can claim a maximum of 8 credits (2 courses) toward both their Exploratory and major requirements. During their final year of study, students have the option to either complete a capstone project, which will count for 8 credits in their major area of study, or fulfill those credits with additional advanced coursework. Students pursuing a double major must complete all requirements for each major. If two majors (or two minors, or a major and a minor) share cross- listed or tagged1 courses, up to 8 credits (two courses) can be used to satisfy both sets of requirements. This limit is per each pair of majors (or major and minor) and does not restrict the total number of double-counted credits or courses a student can claim. For example, a student can major in both A and B and minor in C with a total of 6 double-counted courses: two in A and B, two in B and C, and two in A and C. This rule does not apply to courses that are not cross-listed or

### Question 42: Explain the cross-listed mechanism between courses.  
### Answer: 

The cross-listed mechanism allows students to use up to 8 credits (two courses) from one major to fulfill the requirements of another major or minor, provided that the courses are cross-listed or tagged. This limit applies per each pair of majors or major and minor. However, this rule does not apply to courses that are not cross-listed or tagged. For instance, if major A requires certain courses from major B for its foundation, exploration, or applications, and these courses are not cross-listed between A and B, they do not count toward the 8-credit limit. In interdisciplinary minors, the requirements mainly consist of courses from other majors, which are usually not cross-listed with the minors and are referred to as 'tagged' by these minors. This mechanism aims to provide flexibility in course selection while ensuring that students meet the requirements of their chosen majors and minors.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 20]
and a minor) share cross- listed or tagged1 courses, up to 8 credits (two courses) can be used to satisfy both sets of requirements. This limit is per each pair of majors (or major and minor) and does not restrict the total number of double-counted credits or courses a student can claim. For example, a student can major in both A and B and minor in C with a total of 6 double-counted courses: two in A and B, two in B and C, and two in A and C. This rule does not apply to courses that are not cross-listed or tagged. For instance, if major A requires certain courses from major B for out-of-area foundation, exploration, or applications of the major, and these courses are not cross-listed between A and B, they do not count toward the 8-credit limit. 1 In interdisciplinary minors, the requirements mainly consist of courses from other majors. The information provided does not mention a specific graduation ceremony for CO25. ### Question 44: How does the core quest course selection work to fulfill core credits?  
### Answer: 

The core QUEST courses consist of four specific courses: Quantitative Reasoning for a Digital Age (4 credits), Scientific Inquiry (4 credits), Introduction to Statistics (4 credits), and Programming for Data Science and Visualization (4 credits). These courses are pre-assigned to students in their first year or the first semester of the second year. Class of 2027 students and earlier need to complete these five core courses to fulfill the 20 credits required for the core curriculum. The core QUEST courses help students develop quantitative reasoning, scientific inquiry skills, statistical understanding, and programming abilities for data science and visualization, which are essential for a well-rounded education. Students are not required to choose from different categories but must complete all four courses in the QUEST core group to meet the core credit requirement. Exploratory requirements, on the other hand, involve selecting courses from three categories (E1-Arts and Humanities, E2-Social Sciences, and STEM) to fulfill additional credit hours, but they do not replace the core QUEST courses. The core QUEST courses are mandatory for fulfilling core credits, while exploratory requirements are elective courses that allow students to explore broader areas of study outside their primary field of interest.

### Question 45: Can I take a 300-level course to fulfill a 200-level major requirement?  
### Answer: 

No, you cannot take a 300-level course to fulfill a 200-level major requirement. The context specifies that students are required to take six (06) 200-level intermediate courses for the major. While 300-level courses are considered advanced courses, they do not meet the requirement for 200-level courses. Therefore, it is not permissible to substitute 300-level courses for 200-level courses in fulfilling the major requirements.

### Question 46: If I take a course at another university and want to transfer the credits to Fulbright, but Fulbright doesn’t have that major code, yet the course is related to a major at Fulbright, can I count it toward that major?  
### Answer: 

If you take a course at another university and want to transfer the credits to Fulbright, but Fulbright doesn't have that major code, yet the course is related to a major at Fulbright, you might still be able to count it toward that major. According to the context provided, courses from other majors that are not cross-listed with the minors are referred to as 'tagged' by these minors. Therefore, you could potentially tag the course towards the relevant major at Fulbright, provided that the course content aligns with the major's requirements and meets the necessary standards set by Fulbright. However, this would require submitting an Application for Credit Transfer along with detailed information about the course, including its curriculum, learning outcomes, course content, and assessments. The Office of the Registrar would then coordinate with the Office of the Dean to determine whether credit or an exemption is justified. It's important to note that the maximum number of double-counted credits is often limited, so you should check the specific policies regarding double counting at Fulbright. Triple counting is not allowed.

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 20]

For example, an exploratory course can also be counted as a major course for the corresponding major. However, the number of double-counted credits is often limited (e.g., see subsections 13.2 and 13.3). Additionally, any particular course can only be used to satisfy at most two requirements. For instance, if course X is cross-listed between major A and major B and tagged by minor C, then X can be used to fulfill the requirements of major A and major B, or major A and minor C, or major B and minor C. Triple counting is not allowed. 13.9  Maximal Length of Study Fulbright follows MOET regulations, which state that the maximum time to complete the program cannot exceed double the standard

### Question 47: I have 112 credits from Fulbright and 16 credits from EL. Can I graduate?  
### Answer: 

Based on the information provided, you need at least 128 total earned credits to graduate from Fulbright University Vietnam. You currently have 128 credits (112 from Fulbright + 16 from EL). Therefore, you meet the minimum credit requirement to potentially graduate. However, it's important to ensure that these credits include all required courses and meet the standards set by your major and the university. Consult with your Academic/Major Advisor to confirm that you have fulfilled all graduation requirements. The 16 credits from EL are within the allowed maximum of 12 credits, so they are acceptable towards your graduation requirements. Good luck!

A student wishing to do a capstone must apply during the term prior to the first term of the capstone (Capstone I), and may only apply after having completed 80 credits of coursework. ### Question 49: What is the maximum number of credits I can transfer from another university after officially enrolling at Fulbright?  
### Answer: 

The maximum number of credits you can transfer from another university after officially enrolling at Fulbright is 64 credit hours. This means that to earn a Fulbright University Vietnam undergraduate degree, a student must earn at least 64 credit hours from courses delivered by Fulbright. However, the total number of earned credits must be at least 128 to graduate with an undergraduate degree from Fulbright. Therefore, the 64 credit hours transferred can be part of the required 128 credits, but the remaining credits must be earned through courses delivered by Fulbright.

### Question 50: In a regular semester, can I take only 2 courses? If so, how will my tuition fees be affected?  
### Answer: 

Based on the information provided, you cannot take only 2 courses in a regular semester if you want to maintain full-time enrollment status. Full-time enrollment typically requires registering for a minimum of 12 credits per term. Taking fewer than 12 credits means you won't be considered a full-time student, and as a result, you may lose your eligibility for financial aid. Additionally, there is no reduction in tuition for students whose course withdrawal results in fewer than 12 academic credits. Therefore, if you wish to take only 2 courses, you would need to either find a way to meet the full-time requirement or accept that your tuition fees will not be reduced. Exceptions to this rule might be considered under exceptional circumstances, but these would require approval from the academic advisor, the Dean's Office, and the Registrar's Office, along with supporting documentation. However, such exceptions are rare and typically involve serious medical conditions or other compelling reasons. In summary, taking only 2 courses would likely affect your financial aid eligibility and tuition fees unless you qualify for an exception.

The first period starts after 4:00 PM Friday, Week 2 to 4:00 PM Friday, Week 4. During this time, students can request to withdraw from the course without any consequence. The second period starts after 4:00 PM Friday, Week 4 to 4:00 PM Friday, Week 8. Attempted credits and a “W” grade will be recorded on the transcript for the course(s) from which students withdraw in this period. This grade does not affect a student’s GPA. ### Question 51: Can I pursue 3 majors?  
### Answer: 

According to the information provided, you cannot directly pursue 3 majors. The text states that "Students pursuing a double major must complete all requirements for each major." This implies that pursuing more than two majors would require completing all requirements for each additional major, which is not explicitly addressed in the given context. Therefore, while it's possible to double-major, pursuing three separate majors would likely involve completing the full set of requirements for each, which could be challenging and is not mentioned as a possibility in the provided rules. It's advisable to consult with academic advisors for specific guidance tailored to your situation.

### Question 52: How can I change my major declaration?  
### Answer: 

To change your major declaration, you need to follow the specific policies set by the major coordinator and the Dean’s Office. Typically, changes require careful consideration and approval from your primary advisor. It's important to note that you cannot alter the research question, subjects, or key methodologies without consulting and obtaining approval from your advisors. Additionally, you should ensure you understand the submission requirements at various stages of the project and clarify any doubts with your advisor. For formal declaration of the major, you must complete the core course Design and Systems Thinking, one Mathematics or Basic Science course, and one Engineering course. For the minor, completing the core course Design and Systems Thinking and one Engineering course suffices. If you're considering changing to the History Major, you would need to take alongside two semesters of independent research and writing (2 credits each). The History Major Capstone involves producing a body of original historical research based on primary source evidence, often in the form of an undergraduate thesis or a non-traditional project with a substantial written component. Always consult with your academic advisor for specific guidance and approval processes. The exact procedures might vary depending on the institution's policies and the specific major you are interested in switching to. Ensure you meet all the prerequisites and requirements for the new major before making the switch. Remember, changes to the topic within the major also require careful consideration and approval from the primary advisor. Notably, you cannot change the research question, subjects, or key methodology without consulting your advisors and getting their approval. Clarify with your advisor what you are required to submit at certain stages of the project. If you face initial academic difficulties that make full-time registration unreasonable, you must resume to a full course of study next available quarter. A letter from a licensed medical doctor is required if you are prevented by illness or medical condition from pursuing a full course of study. The letter should include a description of the medical problem, details of treatment, and a recommendation of when the student may resume taking full-time coursework. For the Engineering Major, you need to complete core courses, engineering courses, mathematics or basic science courses, a programming course, and capstone courses. Ensure you meet these requirements before seeking to change your major. Always seek advice from your academic advisor regarding the specific steps and requirements for changing your major. The process typically involves submitting a request to the academic advisor or the Dean’s Office, detailing why you wish to change your major and providing any necessary documentation or letters. They will review your request and inform you of the next steps. Keep in mind that the process can vary between institutions, so it's crucial to follow the guidelines provided by your specific institution. as exceptional circumstances: o submitting the wrong file type or a corrupted file o starting the file upload after the deadline has passed o a claim of technical issues with no proof of an error message or system failure. • Misreading of the assessment or examination timetable • English not being a first language • Deadlines for work or exams being set close together The following may only be regarded as exceptional if there is evidence that they are unforeseeable, unavoidable, and had an impact on the assessment: • Constraints arising from paid employment (full-time students) • Relationship breakdown • Disabilities for which reasonable adjustments have been made • Financial difficulties When there are circumstances beyond the control and need adjustment(s), students may request special considerations. ### Question 54: Which major has the fewest requirements?  
### Answer: 

Based on the information provided, the major with the fewest requirements appears to be the Computer Science major. It does not specify a number of credits or courses that students need to complete, unlike the Art and Media Studies major which requires completing Introduction to Visual Studies and two Foundational courses for the major. Additionally, the Exploratory requirements mention specific credit and course numbers for other majors, but do not for Computer Science. Therefore, Computer Science seems to have fewer specified requirements compared to the other majors mentioned. However, it's important to note that this conclusion is based on the limited information provided and may vary depending on additional unspecified requirements.

### Question 55: How many courses can I take in total?  
### Answer: 

You can take a total of 128 credits. However, this total includes the credits from your majors, minors, electives, and any other requirements specific to your degree program. The information provided does not specify a maximum number of courses, only a total credit requirement. Therefore, the number of courses you can take in total depends on the credit value of each course and whether they meet the requirements for your degree. As long as you meet the credit requirement and adhere to the rules about double-counting and fulfilling requirements, there is no explicit limit on the total number of courses you can take.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 20]
and a minor) share cross- listed or tagged1 courses, up to 8 credits (two courses) can be used to satisfy both sets of requirements. This limit is per each pair of majors (or major and minor) and does not restrict the total number of double-counted credits or courses a student can claim. For example, a student can major in both A and B and minor in C with a total of 6 double-counted courses: two in A and B, two in B and C, and two in A and C. This rule does not apply to courses that are not cross-listed or tagged. For instance, if major A requires certain courses from major B for out-of-area foundation, exploration, or applications of the major, and these courses are not cross-listed between A and B, they do not count toward the 8-credit limit. 1 In interdisciplinary minors, the requirements mainly consist of courses from other majors. There is no general limit mentioned for the total number of courses you can take in a semester. Typically, universities have a standard full-time course load, but this can vary. ### Question 59: If I'm going for a double major then how many courses can be double count maximum?  
### Answer: 

If you're going for a double major, the maximum number of courses that can be double-counted is 8 credits (2 courses) per pair of majors. This means for two majors, you can have up to 8 credits double-counted between them. However, this limit applies per pair of majors and does not restrict the total number of double-counted credits across all your majors. So, if you have a third major, you could potentially have another 8 credits double-counted, making it up to 16 credits in total across all pairs of majors.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 20]
major or minor requirements must be taken for a letter grade. Students can claim a maximum of 8 credits (2 courses) toward both their Exploratory and major requirements. Students pursuing a double major must complete all requirements for each major. ### Question 60: Can you give me the grade translation from alphabetic to numerical?  
### Answer: 

The grade translation from alphabetic to numerical at Fulbright is as follows: 'A' corresponds to 4.0 grade points, 'B+' to 3.3, 'B' to 3.0, 'B-' to 2.7, 'C+' to 2.3, 'C' to 2.0, 'C-' to 1.7, 'D+' to 1.3, 'D' to 1.0, and 'F' to 0.0 grade points. This scale is used unless otherwise decided by faculty in individual courses.

The final grade for the thesis is the average of the 2 graders. The oral defense is graded by a panel of all IS faculty. See Appendix 1and 2 for detail rubrics on both the thesis and presentation. The total grade of the capstone is the sum of the thesis grade and oral defense grade. The primary advisor will communicate this result and any feedback from the graders to the student in writing. If there are any concerns about the grades, students should first meet with their primary advisor to discuss and clarify. If no agreement is made, the student could request one more grader (an IS faculty) to grade their submission, but cannot choose who the grader would be. The major coordinator will decide the 3rd grader after discussion with IS faculty team. The new grade would be the average of the 3 graders. Any further appeal after this would need to follow official UG program’s process for grade appeal.

### Question 62: Why and how am I getting academic suspension?  
### Answer: 

You are getting academic suspension because you failed to fulfill all the requirements of your Learning Contract while on Academic Probation. The process typically involves meeting with the Dean’s Office to establish conditions you need to meet before returning to Fulbright. During this period, you may not visit the Fulbright campus without permission from the Dean’s Office or the Vice President of Student Affairs for relevant meetings. Academic suspension usually lasts for one term or one academic year, and you can be suspended a maximum of two times before your enrollment in the program is canceled, requiring you to apply for re-admission. If you are re-admitted, you must also reapply for financial aid and provide a letter addressing the reasons for your suspension and the measures you've taken to address those reasons. This decision is made by the Dean’s Office in consultation with appropriate faculty and staff members. Academic suspension is a serious step taken when your academic performance is unsatisfactory and puts you at risk of losing the opportunity to study at Fulbright.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 11]

Suspensions are entered on the student’s transcript as part of their academic record. The suspended student must meet with the Dean’s Office, who establishes the conditions that the student must meet prior to returning to Fulbright. While on suspension, a student may not visit the Fulbright campus unless permission is given from the Dean’s Office or the Vice President of Student Affairs for the purpose of attending relevant meetings. DISMISSAL: In all circumstances, Fulbright’s goal is to support students in the completion of their degree. However, dismissal from Fulbright may occur if a suspended student does not fulfill the conditions of suspension, if a student’s behavior represents present harm to others in the Fulbright community, or if a student’s academic performance is unsatisfactory to the degree that continued enrollment is deemed unsupportable. Such decisions are never taken likely, and dismissal occurs only in extraordinary circumstances following multiple written warnings, as determined by the Dean’s Office in consultation with appropriate members of faculty and staff.
